- Size of R&D organization: As of the end of fiscal year 2025, NVIDIA had about 36,000 employees worldwide; about 27,100 of those employees worked in research and development. ([fintel.io](https://fintel.io/doc/sec-nvidia-corp-1045810-10k-2025-february-26-20145-3358))

- Company scale and total revenue: NVIDIA’s total revenue for fiscal year 2025 was $130,497 million (about $130.5 billion). ([fintel.io](https://fintel.io/doc/sec-nvidia-corp-1045810-10k-2025-february-26-20145-3358))

- Data Center revenue (big product outcome): NVIDIA reported Data Center revenue of $115,186 million (about $115.2 billion) for fiscal year 2025. The company showed this growth was driven largely by its GPU and networking products for data centers. ([fintel.io](https://fintel.io/doc/sec-nvidia-corp-1045810-10k-2025-february-26-20145-3358))

- Segment-level revenue showing R&D conversion: NVIDIA’s Compute & Networking segment (the part that includes its data-center chips and networking products) reported $116,193 million in revenue for fiscal 2025, up sharply from $47,405 million the prior year. ([fintel.io](https://fintel.io/doc/sec-nvidia-corp-1045810-10k-2025-february-26-20145-3358))

- Reported cause of data-center growth: NVIDIA’s 10‑K says Data Center computing revenue grew 162% year over year in fiscal 2025 and attributes that growth primarily to demand for its Hopper and Blackwell GPU platforms. ([fintel.io](https://fintel.io/doc/sec-nvidia-corp-1045810-10k-2025-february-26-20145-3358))

- Quarterly evidence of product-driven sales (examples): In NVIDIA’s public quarterly releases, Data Center revenue was reported as $26.3 billion for the second quarter of fiscal 2025 and $30.8 billion for the third quarter of fiscal 2025, showing large, product-linked quarterly sales. ([investor.nvidia.com](https://investor.nvidia.com/news/press-release-details/2024/NVIDIA-Announces-Financial-Results-for-Second-Quarter-Fiscal-2025/default.aspx?utm_source=openai))

- High profit margins on products: NVIDIA reported gross margins of about 75.0% for fiscal 2025, indicating the company sells products and systems at high markups versus their direct costs. ([fintel.io](https://fintel.io/doc/sec-nvidia-corp-1045810-10k-2025-february-26-20145-3358))

- Operating profit scale: NVIDIA reported operating income of $81,453 million for fiscal 2025, reflecting large profits after operating expenses (including R&D). ([fintel.io](https://fintel.io/doc/sec-nvidia-corp-1045810-10k-2025-february-26-20145-3358))

- Market share evidence for product adoption (discrete GPUs): Industry research firms reported NVIDIA holding an extremely large share of the discrete GPU market in 2025 (reports around 92–94% of add-in-board GPU shipments in various quarters), showing wide adoption of its graphics and AI cards. ([jonpeddie.com](https://www.jonpeddie.com/news/q225-pc-graphics-add-in-board-shipments-increased-27-0-from-last-quarter/?utm_source=openai))

- Developer ecosystem and software adoption: NVIDIA’s own product announcements state a developer community of millions (press materials cite a community of over 6 million developers and thousands of CUDA-enabled applications), showing broad software and developer support for its GPU platform. ([investor.nvidia.com](https://investor.nvidia.com/news/press-release-details/2025/NVIDIA-Unveils-Rubin-CPX-A-New-Class-of-GPU-Designed-for-Massive-Context-Inference/default.aspx?utm_source=openai))

- Cloud and enterprise customers using NVIDIA products: Major cloud providers and enterprise partners publicly announce NVIDIA-based offerings and instances (for example, AWS EC2 instances powered by NVIDIA A100/H100 and long‑running collaborations with Microsoft Azure to adopt NVIDIA’s Blackwell and H100-based offerings). These public product listings and announcements show large cloud customers buy and sell services built on NVIDIA hardware. ([aws.amazon.com](https://aws.amazon.com/ec2/instance-types/p4/?utm_source=openai))

- Product and system launches linked to revenue: NVIDIA’s public launch events and product pages for major GPU families (H100, Hopper, Blackwell, and subsequent Blackwell-class systems) are repeatedly cited in the company’s earnings materials and 10‑K as drivers of the large increases in Data Center revenue. ([nvidianews.nvidia.com](https://nvidianews.nvidia.com/news/nvidia-announces-financial-results-for-third-quarter-fiscal-2025?utm_source=openai))

- Investments to support product rollouts: NVIDIA disclosed large purchase and capacity commitments (for example, multi-year cloud service agreements and inventory purchase obligations) and capitalized R&D amounts that increased in fiscal 2025—showing the company invested in production, cloud partnerships, and engineering as it introduced new platforms. ([fintel.io](https://fintel.io/doc/sec-nvidia-corp-1045810-10k-2025-february-26-20145-3358))

- Example customers named in product rollouts: NVIDIA press releases and investor communications name enterprise customers and partners (for instance, early adoption lists for some server GPU products and public collaborations with companies such as Microsoft, AWS partners, and other large technology customers) that adopted NVIDIA’s new data-center products. ([investor.nvidia.com](https://investor.nvidia.com/news/press-release-details/2025/NVIDIA-Announces-Financial-Results-for-Second-Quarter-Fiscal-2026/default.aspx?utm_source=openai))

---

## Point 15 - Question 3

**How effectively does management coordinate multidisciplinary research teams with production and sales groups to ensure new offerings can be manufactured efficiently and meet market needs?**

### Research Findings

- NVIDIA’s reported research-and-development spending for fiscal year 2024 was $8,675 million (about 14.2% of revenue). ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581024000029/nvda-20240128.htm))

- The company says it has invested about $45.3 billion in research and development since it started. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581024000029/nvda-20240128.htm))

- At the end of fiscal 2024 NVIDIA reported about 29,600 employees worldwide. Of those, about 22,200 worked in research and development, and about 7,400 worked in sales, marketing, operations, or administrative roles. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581024000029/nvda-20240128.htm))

- NVIDIA’s public filings say the company relies on outside firms to make, assemble, test and package its chips, and that this reliance reduces NVIDIA’s direct control over quantity, quality, yields (how many good chips are made), and delivery timing. The filings list a small number of key suppliers and geographic concentration of suppliers as risks. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581024000029/nvda-20240128.htm))

- NVIDIA works closely with large semiconductor manufacturers (foundries). NVIDIA has joint projects and production relationships with TSMC (the main foundry for NVIDIA’s AI GPUs). News reports and company announcements say some NVIDIA Blackwell GPUs have been or are being produced at TSMC’s Arizona plant while advanced packaging steps have remained tied to Taiwan facilities. (A “foundry” makes the raw chips; “packaging” is the later step that puts the chip into a finished module.) ([investor.nvidia.com](https://investor.nvidia.com/news/press-release-details/2024/TSMC-and-Synopsys-Bring-Breakthrough-NVIDIA-Computational-Lithography-Platform-to-Production/default.aspx?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA announced a production partnership for a software-to-manufacturing tool called cuLitho that moved from research into production with TSMC and Synopsys on March 18, 2024. cuLitho uses NVIDIA’s GPUs and software to make one step of chip manufacturing much faster, and the companies said it reduced compute time dramatically when integrated into TSMC’s workflow. This is an example where NVIDIA research was integrated with a foundry’s production systems. ([investor.nvidia.com](https://investor.nvidia.com/news/press-release-details/2024/TSMC-and-Synopsys-Bring-Breakthrough-NVIDIA-Computational-Lithography-Platform-to-Production/default.aspx?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA runs reference designs and partner programs so other companies can build systems that use NVIDIA chips. For example, NVIDIA has a longtime HGX/HGX-architecture partner program with large server makers (ODM/ODMs such as Foxconn, Quanta, Wistron, Inventec) so those manufacturers can build and ship servers that use NVIDIA designs. These partnerships connect NVIDIA engineers and product designs to the companies that actually build and sell server hardware. ([nvidianews.nvidia.com](https://nvidianews.nvidia.com/news/nvidia-partners-with-world-s-top-server-manufacturers-to-advance-ai-cloud-computing?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA offers cloud and production services tied to its technology. It launched an “AI foundry” service (with Microsoft Azure) that bundles NVIDIA models, tools, and DGX Cloud computing so customers can develop and deploy AI work. NVIDIA also reports multi‑year cloud-service agreements that it uses to support development and testing. These services link NVIDIA’s research tools and software to cloud providers and customers who deploy products. ([investor.nvidia.com](https://investor.nvidia.com/news/press-release-details/2023/NVIDIA-Introduces-Generative-AI-Foundry-Service-on-Microsoft-Azure-for-Enterprises-and-Startups-Worldwide/default.aspx?utm_source=openai))

- News and analyst reports say NVIDIA has faced supply and ramp-up problems when demand grew very quickly. Reporting in late 2024 noted supply bottlenecks, complex packaging steps, and a design/yield issue that slowed deliveries and forced longer ramp times into subsequent quarters. Those items are described as limiting deliveries even while demand was very high. ([reuters.com](https://www.reuters.com/technology/nvidias-supply-snags-hurting-deliveries-mask-booming-demand-2024-11-21/?utm_source=openai))

- Industry reports and analysts note that advanced packaging capacity (the step after wafer fabrication) is a bottleneck for very large, high‑performance NVIDIA chips. Some press reports say front-end chip production can be moved to new U.S. fabs, but the specialized packaging used for top-end NVIDIA GPUs currently remains concentrated in Taiwan, causing extra logistics and coordination steps. Other firms (including Intel and Amkor) and contract manufacturers have been discussed as additional packaging or assembly partners. ([tomshardware.com](https://www.tomshardware.com/pc-components/gpus/tsmc-is-reportedly-in-talks-with-nvidia-to-make-blackwell-gpus-in-arizona-blackwell-silicon-needs-to-be-shipped-back-to-taiwan-for-assembly?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA’s public presentations and CEO speeches describe the company as a “full‑stack” or “platform” company that develops both hardware and software together (for example: GPUs, systems like DGX and HGX, and software such as CUDA, NeMo, and AI Enterprise). Those messages emphasize that product teams work on silicon, systems, and software as linked parts of a product offering. ([blogs.nvidia.com](https://blogs.nvidia.com/blog/gtc-keynote/?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA’s annual report warns that bringing new products to market and getting “design wins” (agreement from large customers to use a chip design) can be a long process. The company also discloses that changes in cloud services availability, export rules, or supplier locations can change development timelines and make transitions costly and time-consuming. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581024000029/nvda-20240128.htm))

- The company reports significant spending on computing infrastructure for R&D: its SEC filing shows capitalized R&D equipment and multi‑year cloud service agreements used to support development and testing. This indicates NVIDIA invests in tools and cloud capacity to move research into working products. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581024000029/nvda-20240128.htm))

- NVIDIA’s public materials list many external partners across the supply chain and sales channels: foundries (TSMC), packaging and assembly firms, server builders/ODM partners (Foxconn, Quanta, Inventec, Wistron), and major cloud customers (hyperscalers) that take systems built around NVIDIA chips. These partners are part of the path from research to finished products and to customers. ([investor.nvidia.com](https://investor.nvidia.com/news/press-release-details/2024/TSMC-and-Synopsys-Bring-Breakthrough-NVIDIA-Computational-Lithography-Platform-to-Production/default.aspx?utm_source=openai))

- The company’s filings and press releases make repeated, explicit links between new technology work (for example, new GPU architectures and software libraries) and the need to coordinate with manufacturing partners, server builders and cloud providers to achieve volume production and customer deployment. ([investor.nvidia.com](https://investor.nvidia.com/news/press-release-details/2024/TSMC-and-Synopsys-Bring-Breakthrough-NVIDIA-Computational-Lithography-Platform-to-Production/default.aspx?utm_source=openai))

---

## Point 15 - Question 4

**Does management maintain stable, long-term R&D programs, or does it frequently interrupt projects with “crash” initiatives that raise costs and dilute results?**

### Research Findings

- Company: NVIDIA Corporation (ticker: NVDA). Source: company filings. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))

- R&D spending (dollars and change): For fiscal year ended Jan. 26, 2025, NVIDIA reported research and development (R&D) expense of $12,914 million, up $4,239 million (49%) from $8,675 million in fiscal 2024. (Fiscal year 2025 = year ended Jan. 26, 2025.) ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))

- R&D as share of revenue: R&D was 9.9% of net revenue in fiscal 2025, compared with 14.2% in fiscal 2024 (the drop in percent reflects much larger revenue growth in FY2025). ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))

- R&D headcount: As of the end of fiscal 2025, NVIDIA had about 36,000 total employees, of whom approximately 27,100 were engaged in research and development. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))

- Capitalized R&D and development spending: The company reported capitalized research and development expenditures of $6,256 million (as of Jan. 26, 2025) and said increases in R&D expense for FY2025 were driven by a 32% rise in compensation and benefits, a 100% increase in compute and infrastructure costs, and a 234% increase in engineering development costs for new product introductions. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))

- Company statement on R&D approach (platform and multi-year work): NVIDIA’s 2025 Form 10‑K says the company uses a “platform approach” that supports multiple markets from shared technology and that it invests in R&D and may enter multi‑year cloud service agreements “to support these offerings and our research and development activities.” (Form 10‑K, filed Jan. 26, 2025.) ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))

- Public multi‑year product roadmap: At the company’s GTC conference in March 2025 NVIDIA publicly showed a multi-year chip roadmap. Announced and reported items included Blackwell/Blackwell Ultra (shipping in 2025), Rubin (2026), Rubin Ultra (2027) and a later architecture named Feynman (targeted for 2028). Company and press materials show these are planned years in advance. ([theverge.com](https://www.theverge.com/news/631835/nvidia-blackwell-ultra-ai-chip-gb300?utm_source=openai))

- Management on product cadence: At GTC 2025 CEO Jensen Huang said, in public reports, that new NVIDIA products and technologies will come “once a year, ‘like clock ticks’,” describing a regular, planned cadence for releases. ([eetimes.com](https://www.eetimes.com/huang-talks-tokens-reveals-roadmap-at-gtc-2025/?utm_source=openai))

- Long‑running, sustained software and research projects: NVIDIA created CUDA (developer platform for GPU computing) in the mid‑2000s (official public SDK release 2007) and has continued to develop and support it for many years. NVIDIA also launched and expanded platforms such as Omniverse (announced and expanded in 2019–2025) and the DRIVE automotive and Jetson embedded families over many years—these are examples of sustained, multi‑year R&D programs and software ecosystems. ([docs.nvidia.com](https://docs.nvidia.com/cuda/archive/12.9.0/cuda-c-programming-guide/index.html?utm_source=openai))

- Example of a cancelled or changed program: NVIDIA announced in September 2022 that it had cancelled the DRIVE “Atlan” automotive SoC program and replaced it with a new SoC called “Thor” (NVIDIA described Thor as the new road‑map item). Press coverage and company releases document the cancellation and replacement. ([anandtech.com](https://www.anandtech.com/show/17582/nvidia-drops-drive-atlan-soc-introduces-2-pflops-drive-thor-for-2025-autos?utm_source=openai))

- Acquisitions and R&D scale‑ups: NVIDIA acquired Mellanox (a networking and interconnect company) in April 2020 for about $6.9–$7.1 billion; the company has said the acquisition broadens its high‑performance computing and data‑center technology stack. Such deals add engineering and development capabilities. ([nvidianews.nvidia.com](https://nvidianews.nvidia.com/news/nvidia-to-acquire-mellanox-for-6-9-billion?utm_source=openai))

- Recent geographic R&D moves: In 2025 NVIDIA pursued expansion of R&D presence in China, including reports of plans to open a Shanghai R&D center to serve that market while keeping core chip design outside China, as described in press reports (May 2025). ([ft.com](https://www.ft.com/content/c886a4c0-da75-4ea7-8230-6ffd18815fa4?utm_source=openai))

- Management vision and public statements: In 2025 Jensen Huang described NVIDIA’s long-term strategic focus publicly (for example, calling out investment in “physical AI,” robotics and long‑lead data center infrastructure at shareholder and conference presentations). Those talks and the company’s public roadmap presentations signal multi‑year strategic investment priorities. ([observer.com](https://observer.com/2025/06/jensen-huang-nvidia-no-longer-chip-company/?utm_source=openai))

- SEC / risk disclosures about R&D timelines: NVIDIA’s 2025 Form 10‑K warns that investing in R&D in markets with limited history “may not produce meaningful revenue for several years, if at all,” and that timing and availability of cloud services and other factors can affect development timelines—this is a formal disclosure about the multi‑year uncertainty of R&D efforts. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))

- Examples of how product introductions affected costs: The 2025 Form 10‑K attributes part of the FY2025 R&D increase to “engineering development costs for new product introductions,” showing that ramping new product programs was a material driver of higher R&D outlays in that year. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))

---

## Point 15 - Question 5

**To what extent does the company exploit externally funded research (e.g., defense or customer-sponsored projects) to acquire transferable know-how that benefits its own higher-margin commercial markets?**

### Research Findings

- NVIDIA supplies chips and full systems to U.S. national laboratories and government-funded supercomputers. Examples: the Los Alamos “Venado” system uses NVIDIA Grace Hopper Superchips; NVIDIA announced multiple Grace Hopper deployments for national and international supercomputers. ([energy.gov](https://www.energy.gov/nnsa/articles/nnsas-los-alamos-national-laboratory-launches-frontier-ai-models-venado-supercomputer?utm_source=openai))

- U.S. government laboratories (DOE, NNSA, Sandia, Lawrence Livermore, Los Alamos) run joint R&D projects with NVIDIA on hardware and system technologies (for example, memory and packaging research under NNSA/AMT and other collaborations). ([sandia.gov](https://www.sandia.gov/research/news/sandia-partners-with-nvidia-to-advance-memory-technologies-for-high-performance-computing/?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA has won and participated in defense-funded research contracts and programs (historical examples include DARPA awards and DARPA-funded GPU/embedded-processor projects). ([nvidianews.nvidia.com](https://nvidianews.nvidia.com/news/nvidia-receives-darpa-contract-worth-up-to-20-million-for-high-performance-embedded-processor-research-6622726?utm_source=openai))

- Technologies first developed for high-performance or government systems have later been sold or adapted into commercial NVIDIA products. Examples:
  - NVLink was announced for exascale/HPC systems in 2014 and later became part of NVIDIA’s data-center and rack products. ([nvidianews.nvidia.com](https://nvidianews.nvidia.com/news/nvidia-launches-world-s-first-high-speed-gpu-interconnect-helping-pave-the-way-to-exascale-computing?page=318&utm_source=openai))
  - The Grace and Grace Hopper superchip designs used in national-lab machines are also available to commercial system-makers and appear in NVIDIA’s DGX and MGX server specifications. ([investor.nvidia.com](https://investor.nvidia.com/news/press-release-details/2023/NVIDIA-Unveils-Next-Generation-GH200-Grace-Hopper-Superchip-Platform-for-Era-of-Accelerated-Computing-and-Generative-AI/default.aspx?utm_source=openai))

- CUDA (NVIDIA’s parallel-computing platform and APIs) grew out of early academic and research work, including projects that had DARPA support; CUDA then became the dominant software stack that many researchers and companies use on NVIDIA hardware. ([en.wikipedia.org](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CUDA?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA runs funded programs that place external research resources into the academic and research ecosystem:
  - The NVIDIA Graduate Fellowship awards multi‑year PhD research funding (up to about $60,000 per fellowship) and includes an internship with NVIDIA. ([research.nvidia.com](https://research.nvidia.com/graduate-fellowships?utm_source=openai))
  - The NVIDIA Academic Grant Program provides hardware, cloud credits, and software grants to university projects that use NVIDIA technology. ([nvidia.com](https://www.nvidia.com/en-eu/industries/higher-education-research/academic-grant-program/?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA provides, maintains, and open-sources key software and runtime libraries that researchers and customers use in production. Examples include Triton Inference Server (open-source inference server), TensorRT/cuDNN libraries, and newer projects like NVIDIA Dynamo; these software projects are widely used by labs, cloud providers, and enterprises to run models on NVIDIA hardware. ([nvidia.com](https://www.nvidia.com/en-us/ai-data-science/products/triton-inference-server//?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA has many direct customer R&D and co‑development partnerships in commercial sectors (examples from press releases): General Motors (AI for vehicles, factories, simulation), Toyota Research Institute partnerships for autonomous driving, and collaborations with life‑sciences organizations (Illumina, Mayo Clinic, IQVIA) for genomics and drug discovery using NVIDIA platforms. ([nvidianews.nvidia.com](https://nvidianews.nvidia.com/news/general-motors-and-nvidia-collaborate-on-ai-for-next-generation-vehicle-experience-and-manufacturing?utm_source=openai))

- Large cloud providers and hyperscalers that buy or host government‑funded research systems also buy NVIDIA hardware and software (AWS, Google Cloud, Microsoft Azure and others deploy NVIDIA GB200/Blackwell systems in cloud regions). Those cloud deployments are used both by public research projects and by commercial customers. ([nvidianews.nvidia.com](https://nvidianews.nvidia.com/news/nvidia-announces-financial-results-for-fourth-quarter-and-fiscal-2025?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA’s data-center business (the main commercial market for its AI chips) is very large and high-margin in its public financial reporting: data-center revenue accounted for the majority of recent company revenue and gross margins have been in the mid-70% range. (Fiscal 2025: total revenue ~$130.5B, data‑center full‑year revenue reported as ~$115.2B; company-stated GAAP gross margin ~75% for FY25.) ([nvidianews.nvidia.com](https://nvidianews.nvidia.com/news/nvidia-announces-financial-results-for-fourth-quarter-and-fiscal-2025?utm_source=openai))

- U.S. export-control rules and licensing have affected which NVIDIA products can be shipped to some foreign customers (for example, license requirements and restrictions in 2023–2025 on certain advanced AI GPUs to China). NVIDIA reported charges and inventory impacts tied to these export-control developments (for example, an H20-related charge disclosed in Q1 FY2026). ([cnbc.com](https://www.cnbc.com/2023/10/17/us-bans-export-of-more-ai-chips-including-nvidia-h800-to-china.html?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA publicly markets “NVLink Fusion” and similar initiatives to let third‑party chipmakers and system builders integrate with NVIDIA’s interconnect and rack architectures; the company lists partners (MediaTek, Marvell, Fujitsu, Qualcomm, Synopsys, Cadence and others) that are building with NVIDIA’s scale‑up interconnect. These moves formalize industry routes to combine external silicon or customer chips with NVIDIA fabrics and software. ([investor.nvidia.com](https://investor.nvidia.com/news/press-release-details/2025/NVIDIA-Unveils-NVLink-Fusion-for-Industry-to-Build-Semi-Custom-AI-Infrastructure-With-NVIDIA-Partner-Ecosystem/default.aspx?utm_source=openai))

- National-lab and defense projects sometimes provide early testbeds and large-scale operating experience (examples: Venado, multiple Grace Hopper deployments, historical Summit/Sierra collaborations) that show performance, scale, and durability of chips and software before broader commercial rollouts. These projects also create co‑engineering work and close technical feedback loops between NVIDIA engineers and government/science users. ([energy.gov](https://www.energy.gov/nnsa/articles/nnsas-los-alamos-national-laboratory-launches-frontier-ai-models-venado-supercomputer?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA documents and press releases show repeated examples where government or research-funded deployments adopt NVIDIA hardware or software and where NVIDIA then offers the same classes of chips, interconnects, and software to commercial customers and cloud providers (public announcements of lab deployments are mirrored by product availability announcements for commercial systems). ([investor.nvidia.com](https://investor.nvidia.com/news/press-release-details/2024/NVIDIA-Grace-Hopper-Ignites-New-Era-of-AI-Supercomputing/default.aspx?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA makes grants and hardware donations to academic research projects (examples of hardware donations and grant program awards appear in academic announcements and NVIDIA’s Academic Grant program pages). ([icucockpit.com](https://icucockpit.com/2025/03/27/nvidia-gpu-grant.html?utm_source=openai))

- Public reporting and press coverage show that some defense and government-funded projects use third‑party models and software (for example, OpenAI models on NVIDIA‑powered lab supercomputers), indicating industry research ecosystems where commercial AI models, NVIDIA hardware, and national-lab compute are interconnected. ([axios.com](https://www.axios.com/2025/01/30/openai-microsoft-sam-altman-los-alamos-science?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA’s own press releases and product pages explicitly link advances proven in large research/HPC settings (memory technologies, interconnects, chip architectures) to product lines sold to commercial customers, system makers, and cloud providers. ([sandia.gov](https://www.sandia.gov/research/news/sandia-partners-with-nvidia-to-advance-memory-technologies-for-high-performance-computing/?utm_source=openai))

- Many of the specific government-funded projects that involve NVIDIA are publicly documented in lab and agency press releases or in NVIDIA’s news and investor communications; these public records list the systems, the chips used, and the project goals (for example, exascale AI, memory technology research, genomics/health AI platforms). ([energy.gov](https://www.energy.gov/nnsa/articles/nnsas-los-alamos-national-laboratory-launches-frontier-ai-models-venado-supercomputer?utm_source=openai))

---

## Point 15 - Question 6

**Does the organization’s market-research capability prevent significant R&D resources from being committed to technically sound innovations whose addressable markets are too small to earn an attractive return?**

### Research Findings

- Company: NVIDIA Corporation (ticker NVDA). ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581024000029/nvda-20240128.htm))

- NVIDIA’s reported research-and-development (R&D) expense was $12,914 million for fiscal year 2025. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))

- NVIDIA’s reported R&D expense was $8,675 million for fiscal year 2024. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581024000029/nvda-20240128.htm))

- R&D as a share of revenue changed between those years: R&D was 14.2% of revenue in fiscal 2024 and 9.9% of revenue in fiscal 2025 (the percentage fell because revenue grew faster than R&D dollars). ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581024000029/nvda-20240128.htm))

- Number of employees and R&D headcount: as of the end of fiscal 2025 NVIDIA reported about 36,000 total employees, of which about 27,100 were engaged in research and development. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))

- The company describes a formal “platform” approach: it tries to reuse the same underlying processors, software, and systems to serve several large end markets (data center, gaming, professional visualization, and automotive). ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581024000029/nvda-20240128.htm))

- The company’s annual report (10‑K) explicitly says it “has invested in research and development in markets where we have a limited operating history, which may not produce meaningful revenue for several years, if at all.” ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581024000029/nvda-20240128.htm))

- NVIDIA Research (the company’s research organization) lists more than 200 scientists working on long‑term topics such as AI, self‑driving cars, high‑performance computing, graphics, and simulation, and it publishes academic papers and collaborates with universities. ([nvidia.com](https://www.nvidia.com/en-us/research/about/?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA runs developer and startup programs to broaden use of its technology (examples: a large NVIDIA Developer community and the NVIDIA Inception program for AI startups). These programs provide training, SDKs, partner links, and go‑to‑market help for smaller developers and companies. ([developer.nvidia.com](https://developer.nvidia.com/blog/celebrating-the-2-million-innovators-changing-the-world/?utm_source=openai))

- Example of an early, small-market technical bet that later grew into a big platform: NVIDIA created CUDA (its general‑purpose GPU programming platform) in the mid‑2000s, when GPU compute was a small market; CUDA later became a core part of the company’s AI and data center business. ([nvidia.com](https://www.nvidia.com/en-gb/about-nvidia/corporate-timeline/?utm_source=openai))

- Example of an R&D/product area that did not become a large commercial market for NVIDIA: the company bought modem maker Icera in 2011 to support mobile/phone products and then announced a wind‑down of Icera modem operations in 2015 as it refocused on higher‑growth markets. ([nvidianews.nvidia.com](https://nvidianews.nvidia.com/news/nvidia-to-acquire-baseband-and-rf-technology-leader-icera-6622925?utm_source=openai))

- Automotive and robotics programs show long time horizons and continuing investment: NVIDIA’s DRIVE automotive platform and Jetson/Isaac robotics platforms have multi‑year development roadmaps, public OEM design wins, and company statements about multi‑year pipelines and expected future revenue for automotive and robotics uses. ([nvidianews.nvidia.com](https://nvidianews.nvidia.com/news/nvidia-enters-production-with-drive-orin-announces-byd-and-lucid-group-as-new-ev-customers-unveils-next-gen-drive-hyperion-av-platform?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA’s filings say recent increases in R&D spending were driven by more employees and higher compensation, higher spending on compute and infrastructure (cloud/servers), and larger engineering costs for new product introductions. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))

- Capital allocation fact: in fiscal 2025 NVIDIA repurchased large amounts of its stock (the company reported repurchasing $34.0 billion of shares in fiscal 2025 and an enlarged repurchase authorization). This is a public financing and capital‑allocation activity reported alongside R&D and other spending. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))

- The company publishes many product and research announcements (press releases and GTC keynotes) that show simultaneous work on near‑term commercial products (GPUs for data centers and gaming) and longer‑term research projects (Omniverse simulations, robotics models, Earth‑2 digital twin, quantum research center). ([techradar.com](https://www.techradar.com/pro/live/nvidia-gtc-2025-all-the-news-and-updates-from-jensen-huang-keynote-as-it-happens?utm_source=openai))

---

### Point 16 Criterion

Is the company the lowest-cost producer, or at least on par with the lowest-cost producer, for the majority, if not all, of its product lines?

## Point 16 - Question 1

**For each major product or service line, what quantitative or qualitative evidence indicates that the company’s unit production or operating cost is the lowest—or at least on par with the lowest—among competitors? What factors (scale advantages, technology, process efficiencies, supply-chain control) suggest the company can continue to maintain this cost position in the future?**

### Research Findings

Company-wide financial and scale facts
- NVIDIA reported fiscal 2025 revenue $130.497 billion and gross margin 75.0% for the year ended Jan 26, 2025. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))  
- NVIDIA’s annual revenue by end market for fiscal 2025: Data Center $115,186M; Gaming $11,350M; Professional Visualization $1,878M; Automotive $1,694M; Networking (part of Compute & Networking) $12,990M. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))

Data center (AI and cloud) product facts
- Data Center was the largest end market in fiscal 2025 at $115.186 billion in revenue (the major source of company growth). ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))  
- Independent device-count study reported Nvidia shipped about 3.76 million data-center GPUs in 2023, representing the vast majority of industry data-center GPU shipments in that study. ([hpcwire.com](https://www.hpcwire.com/2024/06/10/nvidia-shipped-3-76-million-data-center-gpus-in-2023-according-to-study/?utm_source=openai))  
- Industry reports show NVIDIA capturing very large shares of discrete / add-in-board GPU shipments in 2025 (reported ~92% of AIB shipments in Q1 2025 and similar high shares in other quarters). High unit share implies large production volumes relative to competitors. ([gurufocus.com](https://www.gurufocus.com/news/2913039/nvidia-secures-92-gpu-market-share-in-q1-2025?utm_source=openai))  
- NVIDIA’s Blackwell GB200 NVL72 system achieved MLPerf Training v5.0 results that were up to ~2.6x faster per GPU vs. the previous generation (Hopper/H100) on several benchmarks, and NVIDIA reported the GB200 NVL72 delivered large-scale training and inference speedups in MLPerf submissions. Faster training per GPU directly reduces the time (and energy) needed to reach the same model result. ([developer.nvidia.com](https://developer.nvidia.com/blog/nvidia-blackwell-delivers-up-to-2-6x-higher-performance-in-mlperf-training-v5-0/?utm_source=openai))  
- NVIDIA reported MLPerf Inference v5.0 and Training v5.0 submissions where Blackwell-based systems showed multiplex performance and higher efficiency versus prior NVIDIA generations; MLPerf is an industry benchmark used by cloud and enterprise customers for performance and energy comparisons. ([developer.nvidia.com](https://developer.nvidia.com/blog/nvidia-blackwell-delivers-massive-performance-leaps-in-mlperf-inference-v5-0/?utm_source=openai))  
- NVIDIA sells integrated data-center systems (HGX, GB200 NVL72, DGX SuperPOD). These systems combine GPUs, networking (InfiniBand/Quantum), and software in rack-scale configurations used by hyperscalers; NVIDIA documents per-rack performance and energy-efficiency claims for those systems (e.g., GB200 NVL72 claims of much higher performance per rack and energy efficiency versus prior generations). ([nvidia.com](https://www.nvidia.com/en-us/data-center/gb200-nvl2/?utm_source=openai))  
- Public customers and partners have deployed very large DGX SuperPOD or GB200-based clusters (example: SoftBank announced a DGX SuperPOD with >4,000 Blackwell GPUs), showing demand at hyperscale for NVIDIA systems. Large, concentrated deployments create volume and system-level operating efficiencies for both supplier and customer. ([softbank.jp](https://www.softbank.jp/en/corp/news/press/sbkk/2025/20250723_01/?utm_source=openai))

Gaming (GeForce) product facts
- NVIDIA’s discrete GPU (add-in-board / AIB) market share rose to very high levels in 2025 (reports cite ~92% of AIB shipments in Q1 2025 and similar figures later), driven by the RTX 50-series and other GeForce products; high share in the gaming AIB market implies high unit volumes in that channel. ([gurufocus.com](https://www.gurufocus.com/news/2913039/nvidia-secures-92-gpu-market-share-in-q1-2025?utm_source=openai))  
- NVIDIA reported gaming revenue of $11.35 billion in fiscal 2025 (showing gaming remains a material revenue line though much smaller than Data Center). ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))

Professional visualization and workstation facts
- NVIDIA reported Professional Visualization revenue $1.878 billion for fiscal 2025, up year-over-year, driven by Ada/RTX workstation GPUs used in design, simulation, and generative-AI workflows. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))

Automotive product facts
- NVIDIA reported Automotive revenue $1.694 billion in fiscal 2025, up year-over-year, driven by sales of its Drive self-driving platforms and related systems. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))

Networking, systems, and acquisitions (supply-chain & system control) facts
- NVIDIA completed the acquisition of Mellanox (networking/InfiniBand/Switches) in April 2020; Mellanox technologies (InfiniBand, high-speed Ethernet) were used in many supercomputers and major cloud providers prior to the acquisition. NVIDIA states combining compute and networking improves system utilization and can lower customer operating cost. ([nvidianews.nvidia.com](https://nvidianews.nvidia.com/news/nvidia-completes-acquisition-of-mellanox-creating-major-force-driving-next-gen-data-centers/?utm_source=openai))  
- NVIDIA markets integrated networking and DPU (BlueField) products as part of HGX / HGX-like system designs to enable tighter hardware-software co-optimization across compute, networking, and storage. ([nvidia.com](https://www.nvidia.com/en-us/data-center/hgx/?utm_source=openai))

Software, ecosystem, and developer facts
- NVIDIA states its developer community includes over 6 million developers and nearly 6,000 CUDA applications (company announcements cite the developer count and CUDA application counts as ecosystem metrics). A large software ecosystem means many applications are tuned for NVIDIA hardware. ([investor.nvidia.com](https://investor.nvidia.com/news/press-release-details/2025/NVIDIA-Unveils-Rubin-CPX-A-New-Class-of-GPU-Designed-for-Massive-Context-Inference/default.aspx?utm_source=openai))  
- NVIDIA provides many software stacks (CUDA, CUDA-X libraries, TensorRT, NeMo, NVIDIA AI Enterprise) and publishes performance-optimized libraries and frameworks that customers use to get higher throughput on NVIDIA GPUs. These software libraries are often cited in NVIDIA MLPerf submissions and product materials. ([nvidia.com](https://www.nvidia.com/en-us/data-center/resources/mlperf-benchmarks/?utm_source=openai))

Manufacturing, foundry, and packaging / supply-chain facts
- NVIDIA is a large customer of TSMC. Industry commentary and analyst estimates indicate NVIDIA accounted for a high single-digit to double-digit share of TSMC revenue in recent years and has expanded use of TSMC advanced process nodes and advanced packaging. (Analyst estimates: NVIDIA accounted for ~11% of TSMC revenue in 2023; some broker and press reports discussed NVIDIA taking a large share of TSMC advanced packaging capacity for 2025.) ([tomshardware.com](https://www.tomshardware.com/tech-industry/analyst-estimates-nvidia-is-now-tsmcs-second-largest-customer-accounting-for-11-of-revenue-in-2023?utm_source=openai))  
- Media reports in 2025 cited NVIDIA pre-booking very large portions of TSMC’s advanced packaging capacity (reports quoted >60–70% of certain advanced packaging capacity for 2025 to support Blackwell and related designs). Advanced packaging is required for high-bandwidth memory (HBM) and multi-die designs used in NVIDIA’s largest accelerators. ([investing.com](https://www.investing.com/news/stock-market-news/nvidia-secures-70-of-tsmc-advanced-packaging-capacity-for-2025-taiwan-media-3885209?utm_source=openai))

Benchmark, performance-per-dollar, and energy-efficiency facts that affect operating cost
- MLPerf Training and Inference public benchmark submissions show NVIDIA Blackwell-based systems achieving large multiplicative gains (examples: up to ~2.2–2.6x faster per GPU vs. prior generation on key LLM and image models in MLPerf Training v5.0; MLPerf Inference v5.0 submissions also showed sizable per-GPU throughput gains). Faster throughput reduces compute time and energy for the same work. ([developer.nvidia.com](https://developer.nvidia.com/blog/nvidia-blackwell-delivers-up-to-2-6x-higher-performance-in-mlperf-training-v5-0/?utm_source=openai))  
- NVIDIA publishes system-level energy- and performance-efficiency claims for GB200 NVL72 and HGX platforms (for example, claims of multi-x performance per rack and energy-efficiency improvements vs prior generations on specific workloads). These published system comparisons are used by customers to estimate operating cost per model or per inference. ([nvidia.com](https://www.nvidia.com/en-us/data-center/gb200-nvl2/?utm_source=openai))

Customers, partnerships, and hyperscaler facts (demand and scale)
- Major cloud providers and hyperscalers (examples include Google Cloud, Microsoft Azure, Amazon Web Services, SoftBank, and large cloud-native providers) have announced plans to offer or deploy NVIDIA Blackwell/HGX/GB200-based systems; public deployments and partnerships include large DGX SuperPOD rollouts. Public partnerships and hyperscaler deployments create large, repeatable orders and volume for NVIDIA products. ([investopedia.com](https://www.investopedia.com/nvidia-google-expand-partnership-with-nvidia-blackwell-coming-to-google-cloud-in-2025-8628792?utm_source=openai))

Comparative margin and cost-related facts vs. some competitors
- NVIDIA’s consolidated gross margin for fiscal 2025 was 75.0% (GAAP). By comparison, AMD reported full-year 2024 GAAP gross margin of 49% (and non-GAAP gross margin ~53–54% in quarters of 2024–early 2025), and Intel’s 2025 quarterly gross margins were materially lower in reported quarters (Intel’s quarter-to-quarter GAAP gross margin figures were in the ~27–39% range in 2025 filings). These gross-margin differences reflect gross profit per dollar of revenue and are a financial indicator of pricing power and product mix. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))

Unit-volume and market-share evidence
- Third-party reports and vendor studies in 2023–2025 repeatedly showed NVIDIA dominating data-center GPU shipments and discrete GPU shipments (examples: TechInsights reported ~98% of data-center GPU shipments in 2023; Jon Peddie Research reported ~92%+ of AIB shipments in early 2025). High unit share and shipment volumes are consistent with large manufacturing or procurement scale relative to competitors. ([hpcwire.com](https://www.hpcwire.com/2024/06/10/nvidia-shipped-3-76-million-data-center-gpus-in-2023-according-to-study/?utm_source=openai))

Other supply and production-control facts
- Reports in 2025 indicated NVIDIA had reserved or contracted very large portions of server-assembly and packaging capacity with ODMs and foundry partners to produce Blackwell and related rack-scale systems, and industry articles reported NVIDIA securing major portions of specific advanced-packaging capacity and server plant capacity in Taiwan for its high-end systems. Public company statements and vendor press reported broad OEM/ODM participation in building NVIDIA-certified systems. ([tomshardware.com](https://www.tomshardware.com/desktops/servers/nvidia-books-entire-server-plant-capacity-through-2026-pushing-out-other-potential-customers-to-build-blackwell-and-rubin-ai-servers?utm_source=openai))

Regulatory, export-control, and inventory facts that affect costs and sales
- In April–May 2025 NVIDIA reported U.S. export licensing changes and related charges affecting certain product families (example: a reported charge associated with H20 inventory and purchase obligations was disclosed in May 2025 results), demonstrating that export controls and licensing can affect inventory, sales timing, and near-term operating costs. ([investor.nvidia.com](https://investor.nvidia.com/news/press-release-details/2025/NVIDIA-Announces-Financial-Results-for-First-Quarter-Fiscal-2026/default.aspx?utm_source=openai))

Sources cited above (examples)
- NVIDIA fiscal 2025 Form 10-K and earnings releases (SEC filing, press releases). ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))  
- MLPerf / MLCommons benchmark results and NVIDIA MLPerf submissions, and NVIDIA technical blog posts describing Blackwell MLPerf performance. ([developer.nvidia.com](https://developer.nvidia.com/blog/nvidia-blackwell-delivers-up-to-2-6x-higher-performance-in-mlperf-training-v5-0/?utm_source=openai))  
- Third-party industry shipment and market-share studies (TechInsights, Jon Peddie Research reported in press coverage). ([hpcwire.com](https://www.hpcwire.com/2024/06/10/nvidia-shipped-3-76-million-data-center-gpus-in-2023-according-to-study/?utm_source=openai))  
- Press and analyst coverage of NVIDIA’s foundry and advanced-packaging allocations (TSMC reporting, Investing.com, Reuters/Taiwan press coverage). ([tomshardware.com](https://www.tomshardware.com/tech-industry/analyst-estimates-nvidia-is-now-tsmcs-second-largest-customer-accounting-for-11-of-revenue-in-2023?utm_source=openai))  
- NVIDIA press materials on systems (GB200 NVL72, HGX, DGX SuperPOD) and public announcements about large customer deployments (SoftBank SuperPOD example). ([nvidia.com](https://www.nvidia.com/en-us/data-center/gb200-nvl2/?utm_source=openai))

(End of facts.)

---

## Point 16 - Question 2

**During past industry downturns, did the company remain profitable and/or gain market share while higher-cost competitors curtailed output or exited, demonstrating the leeway afforded by its lower break-even point?**

### Research Findings

- Nvidia’s fiscal year 2019 (year ended Jan. 27, 2019): the company reported full-year GAAP net income of $4.14 billion and positive earnings per share, even though the company said the quarter-ending sales were hurt by “post-crypto excess channel inventory” and weaker end markets. ([nvidianews.nvidia.com](https://nvidianews.nvidia.com/news/nvidia-announces-financial-results-for-fourth-quarter-and-fiscal-2019-6747538?utm_source=openai))

- In late 2018, independent industry data showed total graphics card shipments fell and Nvidia’s share of the discrete (add‑in) GPU market rose to about 81% in Q4 2018 — meaning Nvidia sold a larger slice of a smaller market as crypto‑related demand faded. ([thinkcomputers.org](https://thinkcomputers.org/nvidia-discrete-gpu-market-share-grew-to-81-2-in-q4-2018/?utm_source=openai))

- The 2018 “crypto” downturn: major mining profitability drops led many miners to stop buying or to sell used GPUs, causing big falls in GPU prices and a sharp fall in mining-related GPU demand that year. Companies and market reports described miners exiting or cutting back. ([coindesk.com](https://www.coindesk.com/markets/2018/08/16/nvidia-reports-substantial-decline-in-gpu-sales-to-crypto-miners?utm_source=openai))

- Nvidia’s fiscal year 2023 (year ended Jan. 29, 2023): the company reported GAAP net income of $4.368 billion — down from the prior year but still positive — and said some gross-margin pressure and slower gaming demand had reduced profit compared with FY2022. ([investor.nvidia.com](https://investor.nvidia.com/news/press-release-details/2023/NVIDIA-Announces-Financial-Results-for-Fourth-Quarter-and-Fiscal-2023//?utm_source=openai))

- Industry shipment data in 2022–2023 showed a weak period for discrete desktop GPU shipments (some quarters were the lowest in years). During that period Nvidia’s shipment totals fell year‑over‑year but its market share in the discrete GPU market stayed around ~80% (Nvidia remained the largest seller). ([tomshardware.com](https://www.tomshardware.com/news/gpu-market-healthy-and-vibrant-in-q2-2023-report?utm_source=openai))

- Nvidia’s fiscal year 2024 (year ended Jan. 28, 2024): the company reported a large revenue and profit increase (FY24 revenue $60.922 billion, GAAP net income $29.760 billion), driven by strong growth in data‑center (AI) products. This shows a later period of much higher profit after earlier weakness. ([investor.nvidia.com](https://investor.nvidia.com/news/press-release-details/2024/NVIDIA-Announces-Financial-Results-for-Fourth-Quarter-and-Fiscal-2024/default.aspx?utm_source=openai))

- Competitor inventory and production reactions: Advanced Micro Devices (AMD) disclosed in late 2022 that it took charges (about $160 million mentioned in preliminary Q3 2022 results) mainly for inventory, pricing and related reserves, and said it was working with customers to reduce downstream inventory — an example of a competitor cutting back or writing down inventory during a market weakness. ([ir.amd.com](https://ir.amd.com/news-events/press-releases/detail/1093/amd-announces-preliminary-third-quarter-2022-financial?utm_source=openai))

- Longer‑term competitor exits or product pullbacks: Intel’s first-generation Arc “Alchemist” desktop GPUs (A750, A770) and several mobile Arc SKUs were put on end‑of‑life schedules in 2025, with formal discontinuation notices and final-order dates published by Intel and reported by industry press — an example of a vendor winding down discrete GPU product lines. ([techpowerup.com](https://www.techpowerup.com/338003/intel-arc-alchemist-a750-reaches-end-of-manufacture?utm_source=openai))

- Summary data points on margins and scale (helpful to compare costs and leeway): Nvidia’s reported gross margin moved materially across years (for example, GAAP gross margin ~56.9% in FY23 and ~72.7% in FY24), showing that its per‑unit profit margins can be large when high‑value products (data‑center GPUs) are in demand. (See company fiscal results for exact numbers by year.) ([investor.nvidia.com](https://investor.nvidia.com/news/press-release-details/2023/NVIDIA-Announces-Financial-Results-for-Fourth-Quarter-and-Fiscal-2023//?utm_source=openai))

- Market‑research context: Jon Peddie Research and other industry trackers publish quarterly reports showing that discrete GPU shipment volumes are cyclical (they can fall sharply in downturns) and that Nvidia has frequently held a large share of the discrete GPU market during several down cycles. ([cgw.com](https://www.cgw.com/Press-Center/News/2019/JPR-GPU-Shipments-Down-in-Q4-2018-from-Last-Quar.aspx?utm_source=openai))

---

## Point 16 - Question 3

**Are there explicit cost-reduction programs, continuous-improvement initiatives, or forward-looking investments disclosed by management that reinforce or extend the firm’s low-cost position?**

### Research Findings

- NVIDIA designs chips but outsources their manufacture to third-party foundries (for example TSMC) and uses outside companies (for example ASE, Sigurd) for assembly and testing; the company says it does not operate its own major fabrication plants. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1556898/000095017024032070/ck0001556898-20231231.htm?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA’s 2025 annual report says the company has increased supply and capacity purchases with existing and new suppliers and may enter prepaid manufacturing and capacity agreements to secure future supply. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))

- NVIDIA told investors it plans to increase U.S.-based manufacturing and to invest in specialized equipment and processes to support domestic production and strengthen supply-chain resiliency. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000209/nvda-20250727.htm?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA reported capital expenditures of $3.4 billion in fiscal 2025 (up from $1.1 billion in fiscal 2024) and stated it expects to increase capital expenditures in fiscal 2026 to support business growth. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))

- NVIDIA’s research and development expense for fiscal 2025 was $12,914 million, a 49% increase year over year; management said this rise included much higher spending on compute/infrastructure and on engineering for new product introductions. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))

- In its public remarks, NVIDIA management said they are working to improve production yields for the new “Blackwell” chips, and the company recorded inventory provisions tied to low-yielding Blackwell material while expecting a production and shipping ramp. ([nasdaq.com](https://www.nasdaq.com/articles/nvidia-nvda-q2-2025-earnings-call-transcript?utm_source=openai))

- Management described and promoted software, platform, and service initiatives (examples named: NVIDIA AI Foundry, NIMs, NVIDIA AI Enterprise, NIM Agent Blueprints, Omniverse) that are intended to help customers lower their computing costs and to build recurring software/SaaS revenue. NVIDIA said software, SaaS and support revenue was approaching a $2 billion annual run rate. ([nasdaq.com](https://www.nasdaq.com/articles/nvidia-nvda-q2-2025-earnings-call-transcript?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA cited customer examples where its software/platforms reduced customer costs or cycle times (for example, management said Foxconn used Omniverse to reduce end‑to‑end factory cycle times by about 50%; AT&T reported cost and latency improvements after using NIMs). ([nasdaq.com](https://www.nasdaq.com/articles/nvidia-nvda-q2-2025-earnings-call-transcript?utm_source=openai))

- CEO Jensen Huang and company briefings reported plans for large investments in U.S. supply-chain capacity; press coverage quoted management saying the company plans very large U.S. supply‑chain investments (reported as “hundreds of billions” by the Financial Times). ([ft.com](https://www.ft.com/content/3fd3a717-2fbf-42ef-bb08-5baecdeb1985?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA’s SEC filings state the company’s manufacturing model (using foundries and external test/assembly vendors) reduces the need for large, own‑plant capital spending but also creates exposure to foundry capacity, pricing, and yield risks; the filings warn that foundry capacity is allocated on a purchase‑order basis unless specific agreements are made. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1556898/000095017024032070/ck0001556898-20231231.htm?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA’s sustainability and product statements say improving product energy efficiency is a principal goal of R&D work, and the company highlights energy efficiency as a way to reduce operating cost for customers (noting NVIDIA GPUs power many of the top systems on the Green500 list). ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000086/nvda-20250501.htm?utm_source=openai))

- The company reported entering multi‑year cloud service agreements and partnering with cloud service providers to host NVIDIA services (for example DGX Cloud and AI Foundry), which management presents as a path to recurring, higher‑margin revenue streams tied to its hardware and software platforms. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))

---

### Point 17 Criterion

Is the company willing to change, pivot, and/or restructure itself in response to the ever-changing world around it?

## Point 17 - Question 1

**How frequently and explicitly does management, in recent filings or presentations, acknowledge rapid external change and the need for continual internal reassessment?**

### Research Findings

- Feb 26, 2025 — Annual Report on Form 10‑K (Risk Factors): NVIDIA says its computing platforms “experience rapid changes in technology, customer requirements, competitive products, and industry standards,” and that success depends on the company’s ability to “timely identify industry changes [and] adapt our strategies.” ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))

- Feb 26, 2025 — Annual Report on Form 10‑K (Demand factors): The company lists “rapidly changing technology or customer requirements” among the specific reasons it can under- or over-estimate demand and cause revenue timing volatility. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))

- Feb 26, 2025 — Annual Report on Form 10‑K (Controls/Assumptions): The filing describes that management’s forecasting requires evaluating whether assumptions are reasonable “considering … changes in technology,” showing management documents that its internal estimate process must be reassessed as technology and markets change. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))

- Feb 26, 2025 — Form 8‑K / CFO Commentary (Exhibit 99.2): The CFO’s public commentary on results warns that forward-looking statements depend on “technological development and competition” and other changing conditions, linking financial outlook to external change and management judgment. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000021/q4fy25cfocommentary.htm))

- Feb 26, 2025 — Q4 FY25 Earnings Call (CEO prepared remarks, Jensen Huang): The CEO said “AI is advancing at light speed” and described the company as being “at the beginning of this new era,” language that explicitly calls out very fast external change. ([nasdaq.com](https://www.nasdaq.com/articles/nvidia-nvda-q4-2025-earnings-call-transcript?utm_source=openai))

- Jan–Mar 2025 — Public keynotes and conference presentations (CES 2025, GTC 2025): In public talks, the CEO used similar phrases such as “AI is advancing at an ‘incredible pace’” and “technology is changing the world more quickly than ever,” signaling repeated public statements that the external environment is moving fast. ([blogs.nvidia.com](https://blogs.nvidia.com/blog/ces-2025-jensen-huang/?utm_source=openai))

- Aug 27, 2025 — Q2 FY26 Press Release: The CEO said “The AI race is on,” and described Blackwell as arriving “just in time” as reasoning AI drives large increases in compute — language tying product strategy to fast external shifts in AI model needs. ([nvidianews.nvidia.com](https://nvidianews.nvidia.com/news/nvidia-announces-financial-results-for-second-quarter-fiscal-2026))

- Multiple filings (recent 10‑Ks and quarterly reports across 2023–2025): NVIDIA repeatedly includes risk language saying failure “to meet the evolving needs of our industry and markets” can harm results, and explicitly lists changing industry standards, new product introductions, and competitive actions as items that require the company to change plans and forecasts. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581023000093/nvda-20230430.htm?utm_source=openai))

- Public conference and blog posts (company channels, 2024–2025): Management presentations and blog posts announcing new products and platforms consistently frame the market as rapidly evolving (for example, launching new architectures, networking, and software to meet fast-moving AI demands), showing a pattern of linking product moves to external change. ([blogs.nvidia.com](https://blogs.nvidia.com/blog/gtc-keynote/?utm_source=openai))

- SEC filings and press releases (2024–2025) describing supply and production actions: NVIDIA’s filings note brisk changes in demand and supply (e.g., strong demand for new architectures, supply constraints, increased purchase and capacity agreements), and state the company is increasing purchases, adding suppliers, and adjusting production plans to match changing external conditions. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581024000316/nvda-20241027.htm?utm_source=openai))

---

## Point 17 - Question 2

**What proactive organizational restructurings (e.g., decentralization, segment realignment, new governance layers) has the company implemented in the past decade, and what objectives were cited?**

### Research Findings

- In early fiscal 2021 (reported starting Q1 FY2021) NVIDIA changed how it reports its business into two “reportable segments” named “Graphics” and “Compute & Networking.”  The company said this change matches how senior managers review results and allocate resources, and reflects a shift toward data-center and networking work alongside graphics. ([secinfo.com](https://www.secinfo.com/dvT9t.g5.htm?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA announced and completed the purchase of Mellanox (deal announced March 11, 2019; closed April 27, 2020).  After the deal the company combined Mellanox networking products with NVIDIA’s compute products so it could offer end-to-end data‑center technology (chips, networking, and software).  The company described the objective as raising performance, utilization and scale for next‑generation data centers. ([nvidianews.nvidia.com](https://nvidianews.nvidia.com/news/nvidia-to-acquire-mellanox-for-6-9-billion/?utm_source=openai))

- Following the Mellanox work, NVIDIA created and promoted a BlueField “DPU” product line (BlueField‑2, BlueField‑3, etc.) and associated software (DOCA).  DPUs are network/ I/O processors that take infrastructure work off the main CPU.  NVIDIA said the goal was to speed networking, storage and security tasks, free CPU cores for applications, and make data centers more secure and efficient. ([nvidianews.nvidia.com](https://nvidianews.nvidia.com/news/releases-20210113-6829469?utm_source=openai))

- In March 2021 NVIDIA launched “NVIDIA AI Enterprise,” a packaged software suite and partner program (not a legal reorganization but a formal new product/business initiative).  The stated objective was to let ordinary companies run AI on their existing servers, speed up AI development, and make enterprise IT able to manage AI workloads (including a close integration with VMware). ([globenewswire.com](https://www.globenewswire.com/news-release/2021/03/09/2189505/0/en/NVIDIA-Unveils-AI-Enterprise-Software-Suite-to-Help-Every-Industry-Unlock-the-Power-of-AI.html?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA’s senior leader (CEO Jensen Huang) has publicly described an unusually flat reporting approach: he said in 2023 that he has roughly 50 direct reports.  He explained this is deliberate so there are fewer management layers, information flows faster, and leaders are empowered to act—i.e., an intentional move to flatten the company’s management structure. ([businessinsider.com](https://www.businessinsider.com/nvidia-ceo-jensen-huang-has-50-direct-reports-2023-11?utm_source=openai))

- The company has described “board refreshment” and changes to board committee assignments over recent years (appointment of new directors since 2020, regular rotation of committee membership, and adoption of proxy‑access rules).  NVIDIA said these moves were intended to bring new perspectives, governance skills, and oversight as the company grows into new markets and faces greater geopolitical and regulatory complexity. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581024000104/nvda-20240514.htm?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA’s public filings from 2024–2025 show the “Compute & Networking” segment became the company’s main revenue and operating‑income driver after the segment realignment and after Mellanox and data‑center product ramps.  The filings present this redefined segment as the group that includes data‑center GPUs, Mellanox networking, DPUs, and related software—indicating the company’s objective to organize around accelerated computing plus networking for AI/data‑center customers. ([fintel.io](https://fintel.io/doc/sec-nvidia-corp-1045810-10k-2024-february-21-19774-1729?utm_source=openai))

---

## Point 17 - Question 3

**Which new business lines, technologies, or geographic markets has the company entered ahead of peers, illustrating deliberate pivots rather than defensive reactions?**

### Research Findings

- In 2007 NVIDIA released CUDA, a software toolkit that let ordinary programmers use graphics chips (GPUs) for general-purpose computing instead of only drawing pictures. This made GPUs useful for scientific computing and machine learning. ([developer.nvidia.com](https://developer.nvidia.com/cuda-1?utm_source=openai))

- In 2016–2017 NVIDIA pushed into large-scale AI data centers with purpose-built systems and GPU architectures: it launched the DGX line (a ready-made “AI supercomputer” appliance) in 2016 and the Volta GPU (with specialized “tensor” cores for AI math) in 2017 to speed training and inference. ([nvidianews.nvidia.com](https://nvidianews.nvidia.com/news/nvidia-launches-world-s-first-deep-learning-supercomputer?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA moved from selling individual GPUs to offering full hardware+software systems for AI (GPUs, networking, preinstalled frameworks and appliances like DGX), so customers could buy a complete AI server rather than assemble one themselves. ([nvidia.com](https://www.nvidia.com/en-us/data-center/dgx-a100./?utm_source=openai))

- In 2019 NVIDIA bought Mellanox, a company that makes high‑speed network adapters and switches used inside data centers. That added networking products to NVIDIA’s offerings so it could sell both compute (GPUs) and the data-center networking that links them. ([globenewswire.com](https://www.globenewswire.com/fr/news-release/2019/03/11/1751087/0/en/NVIDIA-to-Acquire-Mellanox-for-6-9-Billion.html?utm_source=openai))

- After the Mellanox purchase, NVIDIA developed BlueField “DPUs” (data‑processing units). A DPU is a network card with a small computer on it that can take networking, storage and security work off the main CPUs, improving data‑center efficiency. NVIDIA announced BlueField‑2/BlueField‑3 and positioned them for cloud and hyperscaler use. ([globenewswire.com](https://www.globenewswire.com/en/news-release/2020/10/05/2103595/0/en/NVIDIA-Introduces-New-Family-of-BlueField-DPUs-to-Bring-Breakthrough-Networking-Storage-and-Security-Performance-to-Every-Data-Center.html?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA created a family of data‑center CPUs and CPU+GPU “superchips” called Grace and Grace Hopper (GH200). These are NVIDIA‑designed processors for servers that share memory with NVIDIA GPUs, aimed at very large AI and scientific workloads. ([globenewswire.com](https://www.globenewswire.com/en/news-release/2022/03/22/2407898/0/en/NVIDIA-Introduces-Grace-CPU-Superchip.html?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA expanded into enterprise AI software and tools (not just chips). It launched NVIDIA AI Enterprise (a certified software stack for running AI on standard servers) and provided toolkits for model building, speech and vision (examples: NeMo, Riva, Clara, Morpheus), so customers could run and manage AI workloads with NVIDIA software. ([globenewswire.com](https://www.globenewswire.com/news-release/2021/03/09/2189505/0/en/NVIDIA-Unveils-AI-Enterprise-Software-Suite-to-Help-Every-Industry-Unlock-the-Power-of-AI.html?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA invested in cloud gaming as a new product line (GeForce NOW). It built and rented GPU capacity to stream PC games to many devices and later struck long-term content and distribution deals (for example a 10‑year agreement with Microsoft to bring Xbox PC games to GeForce NOW). GeForce NOW has been offered in many countries. ([nvidianews.nvidia.com](https://nvidianews.nvidia.com/news/nvidia-introduces-the-next-generation-in-cloud-gaming/?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA pushed into digital‑twin and 3D simulation software with Omniverse, a platform for building and running realistic, shared 3D worlds for design, factories and robotics. It created a purpose-built OVX system (a server design) to run large industrial simulations and partnered with industrial software firms (for example Siemens) to use Omniverse for factory and product digital twins. ([nvidianews.nvidia.com](https://nvidianews.nvidia.com/news/nvidia-launches-data-center-scale-omniverse-computing-system-for-industrial-digital-twins?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA moved into the automotive market with its DRIVE platform (first DRIVE announcements in 2015–2016). DRIVE is an in‑car AI computer and software stack used by carmakers and suppliers; NVIDIA later announced long-term partnerships with many automakers and suppliers to build next‑generation vehicles on DRIVE. ([globenewswire.com](https://www.globenewswire.com/news-release/2015/01/05/1149656/0/en/NVIDIA-Paves-Way-for-Tomorrow-s-Cars-With-NVIDIA-DRIVE-Automotive-Computers.html?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA pursued an “end‑to‑end” data‑center strategy: GPUs for AI, Mellanox networking, BlueField DPUs, DGX systems, and NVIDIA software (CUDA, AI Enterprise, toolkits). The company framed these pieces as a combined platform for cloud, enterprise and research AI workloads. ([globenewswire.com](https://www.globenewswire.com/fr/news-release/2019/03/11/1751087/0/en/NVIDIA-to-Acquire-Mellanox-for-6-9-Billion.html?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA secured commercial partnerships and customer commitments across cloud and enterprise providers (examples: long-term deals or integrations with Microsoft, VMware, Oracle and many cloud providers), showing adoption of NVIDIA hardware/software across major data‑center operators. ([news.microsoft.com](https://news.microsoft.com/2023/02/21/microsoft-and-nvidia-announce-expansive-new-gaming-deal/?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA expanded its product reach into specialized hardware categories beyond traditional GPUs: smart network adapters (ConnectX/SmartNIC), DPUs (BlueField), and full server CPUs (Grace), each announced as distinct product families with technical and customer adoption details. ([nvidianews.nvidia.com](https://nvidianews.nvidia.com/news/releases-20210113-6829469?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA’s technology and products have been adopted in multiple geographic markets and sectors: examples include cloud and AI deployments worldwide (DGX and Grace Hopper supercomputers in Europe, US research centers), automotive projects in China (Baidu) and global automaker partnerships (Mercedes, Toyota and others), and consumer cloud gaming availability in many countries. ([investor.nvidia.com](https://investor.nvidia.com/news/press-release-details/2024/NVIDIA-Grace-Hopper-Ignites-New-Era-of-AI-Supercomputing/default.aspx?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA has repeatedly introduced specialized GPU features for AI (for example Tensor Cores in Volta) and then built software libraries so developers could use those features (cuDNN, TensorRT and the CUDA ecosystem). This created a large developer base tied to NVIDIA’s hardware. ([nvidianews.nvidia.com](https://nvidianews.nvidia.com/news/nvidia-launches-revolutionary-volta-gpu-platform-fueling-next-era-of-ai-and-high-performance-computing?utm_source=openai))

---

## Point 17 - Question 4

**What mechanisms (innovation programs, profit-and-loss responsibility, incentive systems) demonstrate that employees at multiple levels are empowered to propose and execute change?**

### Research Findings

- NVIDIA gives many employees stock awards called restricted stock units (RSUs), performance stock units (PSUs), and market-based PSUs under its equity plans. RSUs usually vest over several years; PSUs only pay out if set performance goals are met. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))

- The company’s filings say PSUs and market-based PSUs vest only when the company’s compensation committee determines performance or market conditions have been met. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))

- NVIDIA operates an Employee Stock Purchase Plan (ESPP). Employees can buy company shares at a discount (price = 85% of a formula based on start or purchase date). From March 2025 the plan allowed employees to withhold up to 25% of pay to buy stock (the Board can change this). ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))

- NVIDIA’s public filings show large stock-based compensation for employees (stock-based compensation expense reported as about $4,737 million for fiscal 2025). This means equity pay is a major part of employee incentives. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))

- News reports described a one-time “Jensen special grant” (April 2024) that gave employees an extra ~25% in RSUs on top of their normal equity refreshes; the reports said the grant vests over four years. ([businessinsider.com](https://www.businessinsider.com/nvidia-jensen-huang-stock-boost-employees-special-grant-2024-4?utm_source=openai))

- In its annual report, NVIDIA says it organizes business into reportable segments and measures each segment by revenue and operating income. The company’s CEO is named the chief operating decision maker (CODM) who reviews segment revenue and segment operating income. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))

- The company publishes operating income by segment (for example, Compute & Networking operating income and Graphics operating income are reported separately in the annual report). This shows the company tracks financial results at the segment level. ([sec.gov](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1045810/000104581025000023/nvda-20250126.htm))

- NVIDIA hosts and runs developer hackathons (for example, DOCA and DPU hackathons). These events invite teams to build prototypes, use NVIDIA tools, and are judged or mentored by NVIDIA engineers and executives. The hackathons are open to a wide range of participants, including developers and partners. ([nvidia.com](https://www.nvidia.com/en-us/events/doca-hackathon/?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA runs GTC, a large developer and research conference. GTC features thousands of sessions, company keynotes (CEO Jensen Huang), hands-on demos and training where engineers, researchers and developers present work and new ideas. ([nvidianews.nvidia.com](https://nvidianews.nvidia.com/news/nvidia-ceo-jensen-huang-and-industry-visionaries-to-unveil-whats-next-in-ai-at-gtc-2025?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA Research operates multiple labs (for example, a Tel-Aviv research lab) that publish papers and describe active research projects. These labs show the company supports employee-led, long‑term research work inside NVIDIA. ([research.nvidia.com](https://research.nvidia.com/labs/par/?utm_source=openai))

- The company runs external startup and developer programs (for example, NVIDIA Inception for startups and developer training through the NVIDIA Deep Learning Institute). These programs give technical help, training, and resources to outside teams and show a company-wide emphasis on building and testing new ideas. ([nvidia.com](https://www.nvidia.com/en-us/startups/?utm_source=openai))

- NVIDIA runs regional innovation and partnership programs and joint research centers with universities (examples include the NVIDIA AI Tech Community and joint centers with Carnegie Mellon University and the University of Pittsburgh). These programs create staffed centers and projects that bring NVIDIA engineers, university researchers, and local partners together to try new ideas. ([blogs.nvidia.com](https://blogs.nvidia.com/blog/pittsburgh-ai-tech-community/?utm_source=openai))

---

## Point 17 - Question 5

**During periods of significant industry disruption or regulatory shifts, what timely strategic actions—such as divestitures, acquisitions, or operational overhauls—did the company undertake, and what outcomes followed?**

### Research Findings

- NVDA is the stock ticker for NVIDIA Corporation. ([investor.nvidia.com](https://investor.nvidia.com/investor-resources/faqs/default.aspx?utm_source=openai))

- In 2006–2007 NVIDIA opened its GPUs to run general-purpose programs (the CUDA software and the Tesla product family). This was a big operational shift from only making chips for graphics to selling chips and software for science, servers and later AI. The change let many researchers and data centers use NVIDIA chips for heavy math tasks. ([nvidia.com](https://www.nvidia.com/en-gb/about-nvidia/corporate-timeline/?utm_source=openai))

- In May–June 2011 NVIDIA bought Icera, a maker of phone modem chips, for about $367 million to try to supply both application processors and modems for phones. In May 2015 NVIDIA announced it would wind down (close) the Icera modem operations and look to sell the technology, saying the company would instead partner with other modem suppliers and focus on higher-growth areas like gaming, automotive and cloud computing. NVIDIA said the wind-down would cause restructuring charges (it estimated $100–$125 million of charges linked to the wind-down at the time). ([nvidianews.nvidia.com](https://nvidianews.nvidia.com/news/nvidia-to-acquire-baseband-and-rf-technology-leader-icera-6622925?utm_source=openai))
